The Road to Civilization . . Arnold S. Turetsky
“We are a pledge to remind the human race of its true goal”
In the plethora of publications which has produced
(Jewishly related) books on imaginary excavations
and real neuroses, the secularism of religionists and
the piety of scientists, expect soon the appearance
of a book having to do expressly with interesting
customs which are no longer practiced. These will
be derived not only from biblical or talmudic pe¬
riods, but from the era of Jewish life in eastern
Europe just concluded, an influence to which we
are inevitably drawn.
Modern readers will find amusement in the un¬
sophisticated naivete of many of these old practices.
Some will seem purely superstitious, others deeply
pious. Under the heading "High Holyda ys", the
author will describe a procedure so simple and lovely
that its extinction presents a discomforting problem.
On erev Yom Kippur it was the custom of the
Jew to walk through the village, calling for a few
moments at the home of each of his relatives and
acquaintances, to ask forgivness for any wrong he
had done them during the year, knowingly or un¬
knowingly. Understanding that the service of Yom
Kippur atones only for the trespasses between man
and God, he was especially anxious that his earthly
relations be at peace before approaching the great
Judge.
This is the custom. Unphilosophical, nonmystical,
a plain expression of human dignity and love. It is
no longer practiced. Consider the development of
human values indicated, and the unfortunate impli¬
cation. Is this custom obsolete or is it too advanced
for our time?
Can you see the members of this Jewish com¬
munity busy two days before Yom Kippur making
RABBI ARNOLD S. TURETSKY
calls, personally and by telephone? This would be
the annual season for mending relationships. Pre¬
tenses are dropped in the sober atmosphere of an
occasion characterized by the prayer for life itself.
Earnestly and sincerely we ask forgiveness of one
another. We look into the eyes of people whose
closeness we cherish, against whom we have sinned.
"Forgive me for the jealousy I have felt, for the
coolness I have shown, for the deceit I have spoken
to you. Forgive my impatience, intolerance, selfish¬
ness. I am guilty of distrust and petty suspicion.
I have slandered you and even hated you. Truly I
beseech your forgiveness. As for you, with all my
heart I forgive you." Imagine this happening every
year, in every Jewish community.
To you, your dear ones, and all your fellow men,
the full ness of God's blessings for a year of good
health, peace, and enduring love.
11
===== PAGE 15 =====
CONSERVATIVE JUDAISM
IN THE UNITED STATES
... AND IN THE WORLD
The Jewish Theological Seminary is training
rabbis for the Conservative and dominant segment
of American Judaism. It is a seminary flourishing
between two Jewish worlds, the world of the Orth¬
odox Jew, committed to unchanging tradition, and
the world of the Reform Jew, committed to a tradi¬
tion of change.
From its cramped corner establishment at
122nd Street and Broadway in New York City,
the Seminary has dug deep American roots for its
ancient religion, and its graduates embody a dual
commitment to their Jewish and American identi¬
ties. It is the clear intention stated in its charter
of 1886: “the preservation in America of the knowl¬
edge and practice of historical Judaism.
With 750 affiliated rabbis directing Jewish
congregations, the Seminary stands at the heart of
Conservative Judaism; from the Seminary emerges
the very embodiment of the American clergyman,
a concerned, committed minister of God trying to
reinforce religious values in a secular environment.
Before admission to three years of graduate
study leading to ordination as a rabbi, the students
must spend one to three years in the preliminary
School of Judaica, where they concentrate on text
studies in Hebrew, the Bible, and the Talmud.
That intensive training period makes up deficien¬
cies in a student body of college graduates with a
variety of backgrounds.
They come from all sections of the country and
from such colleges as Columbia, Cornell, North¬
western, Minnesota, Boston University, Brandéis
and Brooklyn College — as did last fall's entering
class of twenty-one rabbinical students.
At the Seminary, the least forgivable sin is
poor scholarship, and as testimonials to this atti¬
tude, the faculty comprises an awesome group of
Jewish scholars. One third-year student recalled
his first Sabbath service at the Seminary: “I
looked with awe from the back of the room, real¬
izing that in the first three rows, probably the
greatest collection of Jewish scholars in the world
was sitting together and pra3dng!”
12
===== PAGE 16 =====
No simple formula describes these future rabbis
. . . one motivation is universal. They are deter¬
mined to preserve the Jewish identity. They share
a deep conviction that religious Jews have some¬
thing important to contribute to America, and
they don’t want their fellow Jews to disappear in
the American melting pot.
Dr. Louis Finkelstein, the Seminary’s Chan¬
cellor and President and probably the most im¬
portant voice in American Judaism, refers to Jews
as the “witness people.” He described his feelings
after attending the coronation of Pope Paul VI
as a member of the official five member U. S. dele¬
gation. The first rabbi ever to fill such a function
reminded his students and faculty after his his¬
toric experience:
“Our ancient teachers were right in their ad¬
monition that there is a great world contribution
to be made only by those without power, who are
few in number, always a minority, uncertain of
their ability to meet alone the challenge of the
world . . . We are a pledge to remind the human
race of its true goal.”
Overall, the curriculum embraces traditional
studies in the Bible, Talmud, Hebrew, Codes of
Law, and Midrash (the method of using Scripture
as a source of instruction) and study of the broad
field of Jewish culture in history, philosophy, and
literature. But the Talmud is still king at the
Seminary, and even critics of the curriculum agree
that without the Talmud Judaism is not Judaism.
It is no surprise, then, that Dr. Saul Lieberman,
the world’s leading talmudic scholar, is the most
important man at the Seminary. Dr. Finkelstein
may be the Seminary’s brilliant spokesman, but
within its winding corridors, Dr. Lieberman wears
the crown of scholarship.
In the Library, whose 200,000 volumes and
10,000 manuscripts constitute the largest collec¬
tion of Judaica ever assembled, the librarian can
trace the wandering of Jews through the centuries
by telling the stories of individual manuscripts.
A book of prayer dates 1286 in German turned
up in Spain only to depart with the expulsion of
the Jews in 1492, moving to Egypt and making
the round trip to Germany in 1928. The manu¬
script was taken to Holland in the thirties, then
to England, and finally to America, where so
many Jewish scholars have found a haven.
Because of its crucial position, the Seminary
strikes out in many directions. Soon it will have
most of the important Jewish manuscripts in the
world available in microfilm. It built up the Jew¬
ish Museum collection, now housed on New York’s
Fifth Avenue. To train teachers in the Jewish
field, there is the Teachers’ Institute and Semi¬
nary School of Jewish Studies and the Women’s
Institute. The newest collegiate department — es¬
tablished in 1952 is the Cantor’s Institute and the
Seminary College of Jewish Music.
Excerpted from an article by
Edward Wakin in “The Sign”
vU ' vV > j *
V V V ri' V
On Sunday, February 23, 1913, in the large
assembly hall of the Jewish Theological Seminary,
Dr. Solomon Schechter called the United Syna¬
gogue of America into being.
“Blessed be he that cometh in the name of
the Lord” he welcomed the delegates to the or¬
ganizational meeting.
“It is in the name of the Lord that we have
invited you to join the United Synagogue of
America, which is entering upon its existence this
day. It is a real 'work of Heaven’ for which
I invite your attention and participation — a work
on which, in my humble opinion, depends the con¬
tinuance and the survival of traditional Judaism
in this country.”
Today we have 800 Congregations in the United
States and Canada, affiliates of the United Syna¬
gogue.

Being considered one of the outstanding Synagogue
Centers in the Southeast, we provide our membership
a dynamic and diversified program in every phase of
religious, educational, social, cultural and athletic ac¬
tivity. In glancing through our annual Year Book, a
brief summary in pictures and words depicts our year-
round activity with a view of our over-all program.
Your comments concerning our operation and your
continued support are always solicited.
Special thanks are due to our office personnel who
carry out the many details of your Synagogue Center
administration. My personal appreciation to Mrs. Ann
Stegman, Mrs. Florence Robin and Mrs. Esther Fish¬
ier for their devoted and untiring efforts on behalf of
our synagogue.
On behalf of the entire administrative staff we take
this opportunty of extending to our entire member¬
ship a Happy and Prosperous New Year.
ROBERT FISCHER
Executive Director
Robert Fischer, Executive Director
JSrief Scop
Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morning and evening
in the Chapel. Friday Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are observed in
the Sanctuary throughout the year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are observed in connec¬
tion with Circumcision, naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah,
Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion of Judaism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with outstanding program of secular
and religious content. Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive Hebrew content.
Sunday School, with a curriculum including Jewish history and customs, Solomon
Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through 5th grade.
Supplementary religious educational training is practiced in Pre-Jr., Junior, Teen
and Confirmation Congregation services and Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library - outstanding collection of juvenile and adult Jewish
books; Adult Education - courses on religious and cultural Jewish subjects; Music -
a program of religious and cultural Jewish music; Sisterhood and Men’s Club render
valuable service to the Center and its members; Social Activities provided by Mr. and
Mrs. Club, Amity Club, “32 Club”, and Knights and Dames; Family Recreation Pro¬
gram (Olympic Pool), summer week-days and Sundays for all ages at our Campsite.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth, United Synagogue Youth, various age
levels are served by Center Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Major’s Pre-Teens,
Tween-Agers; Scouting - Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts, Brownies and Girl Scouts; Athletics -
programs for youth and adults; Day Camp - A nine week summer program at our 36
acre Campsite.
Family Recreation Program — Daily and weekends at Center Campsite . . . Athletics,
swimming, and snack bar is available during the summer months.

===== PAGE 20 =====
A Message
One of the more important achievements of our
congregation during the past year has been our in¬
creased support of the Joint Seminary Campaign, the
chief beneficiary of which is the Jewish Theological
Seminary.
By now, most of the members of our congregation
realize that we are one of the leading synagogues in
the country. We have constantly intensified and im¬
proved our entire program to the point that it is recog¬
nized as one of the outstanding of its kind in the coun¬
try. The one area where our achievements have not
been outstanding, however, has been the area of sup¬
port for the Jewish Theological Seminary.
Fortunately, during the past year considerable
progress has been made in correcting this situation.
The Board of Directors unanimously adopted the Na¬
tional Enrollment Plan, which involves an assessment
of $10.00 on each member of the synagogue. Even
though the assessment was made late in the year, the
response of the congregation to this appeal was indeed
encouraging. It is hoped that the response during the
coming year will far surpass this initial effort.
In addition to the N.E.P. program, we conducted
for the second consecutive year a special solicitation
for larger contributions, the results of which exceeded
those of the previous year. Much credit for this suc¬
cess goes to Mr. Jack Becker, Chairman, and Mrs.
Joseph Wilensky, Co-Chairman of the committee. The
effort of the Sisterhood in its Torah Scholarship Cam¬
paign also exceeded the achievements of the past years.
Despite these improvements, we must do much
better. “But why?”, one might ask, especially in view
of the considerable other demands which are con¬
stantly being made upon the members of the Center.
Our congregation has enjoyed to the fullest its
participation in all phases of the movement, whether
it was the national and regional conventions, United
Synagogue Youth, Camp Ramah, Cavalcade lecture
series, etc. All of the Rabbis of our synagogue over
the past 40 years, with one exception, have been or¬
dained by the Jewish Theological Seminary. Most of
our textbooks are the projects of the intensive re¬
search and creativity of the United Synagogue Com¬
mission on Education.
More important, however, than the feeling of
obligation for past benefits should be our deeper con¬
cern for the future of Judaism in America. The great¬
est single force determining this future is Conserva¬
tive Judaism, and it is completely tied in with the
future of the Seminary. Whether or not the present
shortage of Rabbis and teachers will be alleviated
depends almost entirely upon the number of Rabbis
who will be ordained by the Seminary and the number
PHILIP SELBER, President
of teachers who will be graduated from its Teachers’
Institute.
Some congregations might attempt to “sluff off”
their more indirect responsibilities such as the Semi¬
nary in favor of their more direct pressing local needs
but a mature responsible congregation, such as ours,
must recognize all of its obligations and give to each
the priority it demands. Let us hope that the coming
year will witness an increased awareness of the needs
of the Joint Seminary Campaign and a greater dedi¬
cation to this cause by both the leadership and the
membership.
On behalf of the officers and members of the
Board of Directors and the Board of Trustees, may
we extend greetings to all of our members for a Happy
New Year, in the traditional words of “LESHANAH
TOVAH TIKATEVU”.
BURTON I. GROSSMAN
Chairman of the Board

To maintain the vast building which the
Center program uses for its many daily func¬
tions, we have the bus drivers listed below and
the staff pictured at the right, in addition to
part-time help composed of Frank Edwards and
Howell Toole.
These veterans and newcomers to the Cen¬
ter operation devote countless hours to seeing
that all functions are prepared for in the best
possible manner.
The kitchen is under the supervision of
Annie Mae Fort who has prepared tasty meals
for our children and members alike for the past
19 years. Also preparing tasty food at many
of the Center’s affairs and members’ Simhas are
two local caterers Elsie and Albert Meyerhoff
and Mrs. Mary Safer whose meals speak for
themselves.
Our appreciations to this loyal and devoted
maintenance, transportation and kitchen staff
who have given many years of unselfish service
to our Synagogue-Center complex.
Our loyal maintenance and kitchen crew as pictured serving a Board
Dinner. From left to right, Wilhelmina Scrivens, Annie Mae Fort,
Leroy Vaughn and Tim Riley.
nr
<J r
ransportatiopi
To transport our students from their schools and
homes to classes at the Center, our buses are con¬
stantly used to relieve the parents of driving to and
from the Center. Commencing in 1965-66 our buses
will provide transportation for Saturday return also
and Sunday morning pick-up for students attending
at 9:00 o’clock classes.
To make our transportation system operate as
safely as possible, we have Morris Montford with over
20 years employment with the Center; Eugene Davis,
a fifteen year veteran with our organization and Willie
Baldwin, who is now in his fourth year with the Center.
Filling in during peak hours in driving the buses, our
capable maintenance staff: Tim Riley, a veteran of
almost 20 years with the Center, and a newcomer,
Leroy Vaughn. The transortation system of routes
and maintenance is under the supervision of our
Transportation Committee, Herbert Lewitt, Chairman,
Lewis Ansbacher, Co-Chairman.
Left to right: Our station wagon used for small pick-ups and our three 66 passenger
buses and the drivers: Willie Baldwin, Morris Montford, and Eugene Davis.

THE CENTER CHORAL SOCIETY PICTURED AT THE ANNUAL CONCERT
The 17th annual Concert of Jewish Music
was presented on January 11, 1965 and
featured the internationally famous Cantor,
Jacob Barkin, as guest artist. The “Hav-
dalah’' by Zilberts was the highlight com¬
position. This annual event is one of the
highlights of our congregation’s calendar.
A large audience enjoyed this program.
This year’s Concert proceeds were divided
between the Sisterhood and the Cantors’
Institute of the Jewish Theological Semi¬
nary of America.
Our junior choral group, the Glee Club,
is composed of members of our Religious
School and performs at various Holidays
in the year and at the Annual Concert. The
Choral Society and Glee Club are under
the direction of Cantor A. Marton.
Members of the Choral Society
Participating in the Concert

Mrs. Louis Slott and Mrs. Helman Setzer
at monthly luncheon meeting.
Torah Fund affair at home of
Mrs. Herbert Lewitt
The year 1964-65 began with two highly successful events
during the first week of October. On October 5th, a delightful
Simhat Torah Tea was hostessed by several of our Sisterhood
members at the home of Mrs. Herbert Lewitt. Our State Pres¬
ident from Miami, Fla., Mrs. Harold Reinhard, and Rabbi
Turetsky were guest speakers.
Two days later a Fashion Show and Membership Tea
were the main attraction, gathering a large number of our
members for a beautiful afternoon of dress, fur and hat
fashions. This was chaired by Mrs. Mortimer Evans and Mrs.
Jack Price. The beautiful models were Mrs. Isadore Mosco-
vitz, Mrs. Melvin Newman and Mrs. Harris Shafton. Mrs.
Manuel Katz introduced the new members in a lovely cake¬
candlelighting ceremony.
At the November 4th meeting Rabbi Turetsky personally
narrated a narrative he had written, with tabloids staged by
our Program Chairmen.
The Hanukah Fair was a huge success. Foods, games and
white elephants were available to all, and game booths were
set up by the Center Youth groups.
The annual Paid-up Dinner Affair program was “The
Three Gifts”. It was presented beautifully under the direction
of Mrs. M. J. Reiser .
An Oneg Shabbat was held by the Sisterhood with the
Library Committee on February 20. Miss Minnie Schreiber
spoke to the group about our excellent Library and Mrs.
Sidney Entman presented a book review.
The closing luncheon featured “Mother’s Day” with prizes
given to the oldest mother, the mother with the most children
and the youngest mother. Mrs. Norman Slaff, President,
thanked Mrs. Lewis Ansbacher, outgoing Cultural Vice-Pres¬
ident, Mrs. Leo Jacobson and Mrs. Herbert Plotkin, outgoing
Program Chairmen, for their three year’s work in their offices.
She then announced that Mrs. Sheldon Gendzier had ac¬
cepted the office of Cultural Vice-President for next year. A
musical program was presented by Mrs. Frank Rosenberg and
Miss Rita Weinstein.
The annual Florida Branch Conference was held in Jack¬
sonville on May 10, 11 and 12 with Mrs. Louis Slott and Mrs.
Nathan Becker in charge of all arrangements. Mrs. Joel Fleet
presented a workshop on Israel affairs. Everyone agreed that
this was an outstanding Conference. The 1966 Conference will
be held at the Deauville Hotel in Miami Beach on May 2-4.
Sisterhood works very closely with our two United Syna¬
gogue Youth groups and sponsors the Girl Scouts and Brownie
Troops. This is the second year that we presented four scholar¬
ships for our youth to attend Camp Ramah. Funds which en¬
able us to fulfill our obligations to the Center are obtained
from the Gift Shop, sale of Candy, World Book Encyclopedia,
Leagrams and your Sisterhood dues. This year we are start¬
ing another beautiful family tablecloth and a cookbook with
recipes from our membership.
We are looking forward to another successful year with
your continued support. Please join us at all meetings. We of
Sisterhood wish you and your loved ones a very good year.
Sisterhood Concert Committee pictured at the Annual Concert on Jan. 11, 1965

===== PAGE 48 =====
Camp Ramah
Camp Ramah began in 1947 as an experi¬
ment, piloted by a group of visionary pioneers,
who took the chance of enrolling 93 youngsters
the first season. Since then it has grown to a
network of six camps spread across the United
States and providing an experience of traditional
Jewish living in an atmosphere of normalcy, for
eight weeks each summer, for more than 2,000
youngsters from Conservative congregations. Ra¬
mah, no longer an experiment, is perhaps the
most dynamic movement in the Conservative
program for youth. Besides the Gordon award,
the Center offered eight scholarships for the
1965 summer season to High School students.
Mr. Alan Gordon presents a certificate of the Gordon Leadership Grant
to Sharon Raskin as Rabbi Turetsky looks on. Mrs. Samuel Gordon,
not in the picture, resides in Miami.
The following are the enrollment require¬
ments for Camp Ramah: Campers must be at
least nine years of age on opening day of camp;
all campers must be pursuing a Hebrew study
program at an accredited Hebrew School or its equiva¬
lent; Camp Ramah requires a transcript of the current
year's Hebrew school record before taking final action
on an application; all campers must be taking Hebrew
work appropriate to their age level and applicants
of high school age must take a written examination.
The students who attended Camp Ramah from
the Center this past summer are: Palmer — Carol
Gross, Barbara Talpalar and Barry Turetsky; Wing-
dale — Martin Berman, Ricki Gross, Charles Katz,
Donna Price, Sharon Raskin, Paula Saraga, Harry
Sinoff, Carolyn Steiner and Todd Price; Nyack — Ira
Brukner.
Our junior Minyan Club (pre-Bar Mitzvah age), which is under the
supervision of Leon Zucker. This group meets every Sunday at 9 a.m.
in the Synagogue. Jeffery Morris was named the outstanding student
of the junior Minyan Club.
M inyan Clubs
The two Sunday morning Minyan Clubs pic¬
tured on this page are made possible through the
combined efforts of the Education Department and
the Men's Club Minyan Committee who assist in
the serving of breakfast. Loyal members of the
Men's Club who have worked diligently on this
project are Morton Harris, Bernard Heyman, Dave
Silver and Phil Bernstein.
The senior Minyan Club is under the direction of Cantor A. Marton for all boys who have been Bar Mitzvah. This group
meets weekly in the Assembly Hall, with breakfast and Sunday School classes following. An award was given to Leon Talpalar
as the outstanding student in this Minyan Club with honorable mention going to Jack Bettman.

mon Schechter. He also emphasized the need for Con¬
servative Jewry to support the Jewish Theological

“Fifty years ago, Dr. Schechter expressed his faith
that in this great, glorious, /ree country we Jews need
not sacrifice a single iota of our Torah; and in the
enjoyment of absolute equality with our fellow citizens
we can LIVE to carry out those ideals for which our
ancestors so often had to die.”
Simon Greenburg
V
In 1957 a World Council of Synagogues was pro¬
posed. Rabbi Charles Lehrman, Chief Rabbi of the
Grand Dutch of Luxembourg stated:
“I appeal to you to spread your wings over the
ocean, continue your amazing growth from a national
to a worldwide movement . . . Bring us your experi¬
ence of creative adaptation to modern conditions of
life. Yes, I endorse here the theme of this Conven¬
tion — ‘lengthen thy cords’ across the ocean.”

berships and rentals for private parties, organizational
gatherings and generally strives to promote the use of

The Tofield Memorial Library has a collection
of almost 3,000 books in English, Hebrew and Yiddish.
There is a wide selection of books of Jewish content
for young and old, for pleasure and study, for reference
and research.
The Library is open Monday through Thursday
from 10 A.M. to 5 P.M. and on Wednesday evening
in conjunction with the Adult Institute program.
A new project started this past summer was pro¬
viding books for the campers during the summer.
An “Oneg Shabbat” featuring the books of the
Library was held with the Sisterhood in January of
this year.

A dictionary gives concise and correct definitions. Popular
notions, however, are not always correct or definitive. The
following represents some statements concerning the meaning
of the word “Synagogue.” Underline your choice. Which would
he your definition?
1. A synagogue is a place to which I send my children. It’s
good for them.
2. A synagogue is like a clinic. You need it for emergencies.
Even if nothing happens there, except in an emergency —
it’s worth having.
3. A synagogue is a fine place for my parents to go. Keeps
them occupied. It’s good for other people’s parents too.
4. A synagogue is an organization I have to join when my
son gets near Bar Mitzvah age. The time element is important.
I join only when I “have to.”
5. A synagogue is a good place to rent for my family cele¬
brations. And when I rent it — I take over! Don’t want to be
bothered about rules and regulations.
6. A synagogue is a business proposition. If education is
costly — cut it down! If expansion of facilities and activities
mean that I have to extend myself too much then I’ll stand
pat and talk about the “high cost of religion.”
7. A synagogue is a membership organization, not a com¬
munity. Once I pay my dues (which in most cases doesn’t
amount to 10 per cent of the money spent on refreshments and
entertainment), I’ve done my share. Don’t tell me about carry¬
ing the share of those in the community who can’t. Don’t tell
me that a membership in a synagogue amounts to good citizen¬
ship in a community, and that I have to bear my just share
of the cost of its operation.
8. A synagogue is a community responsibility to be demo¬
cratically administered. It should reflect the wants and needs
of our people and be regarded as the central institution in the
Jewish community. It is a public trust and a public utility
which I support, not as a matter of philanthropy but as a
civic responsibility! I maintain it in dignity because I parti¬
cipate in its life.

ATID- C oHcg¡e ^\joutlr
ATID, organized in July of this year, is the newest
addition to the organizational ladder of the Center.
Under the chairmanship of Dr. Harry Yergin, and with
the guidance of Mr. Philip Selber and Rabbi Turetsky
preliminary plans for activating such a group were
discussed early in the year.
The first meeting of the new group was called for
June 14 at the Center Campsite and the size of the
attendance plus the enthusiasm of the college students
were encouraging evidence that the project would be
be a fruitful one. A series of weekly discussion meet¬
ings on subjects of contemporary interest to Jewish
college students was planned. At a different home each
Tuesday evening the members met for an evening of
intellectual exchange and refreshment and from this
initial programming more ambitious projects have
developed.
Study retreats, periodic news letters to all members
during the school year and additional opportunities
for discussion forums are among the future plans of
our ATID group.
In August application was made to the national
ATID, the college age affiliate of the United Syna¬
gogue of America, for affiliation and the Center group
is now being chartered.

"The intellectual tension at the Seminary
is in keeping with the passionate Jewish commit¬

three books contain the legal, historical, and
ethical writings of the ancient rabbis, it is writ¬

the focus on study for study's sake comes the
orientation of the Seminary."

===== PAGE 78 =====
Religious Education
TED GROSS, Principal
One of the important long-range goals of religious education is develop¬
ment of disciplined behavior. We are living in an age when mass hysteria
is excused on a basis that this is a way young people let off steam. In an
era when Jews have attained a cultural percentage of one third despite a
numerical minority, the signs, unfortunately, indicate that American Jews
are on the decline. The religious school, therefore, has a double role: of
building disciplined standards for the teen-age youth and a greater sense
of loyalty to the values of our tradition.
The beauty of our tradition is shown by significant innovations in the
intensive programs of our school department.
The formation of a 4th and 5th grade for our Foundation School, which
will now be known as the Solomon Schechter Day School shows that our
parents realize the value of day by day Jewish living. They have special
pride in seeing the joy these children feel in their religious as well as secular
activities of ths department.
JACK
SHORSTEIN,
Chairman
MRS. HANA
ROSENZWEIG
Secretary
Education can often be a more meaningful inspiration when it can take
advantage of a difficult situation and turn it into a positive factor. Since
there is no official Torah reader for the Congregation, the school has intensi¬
fied the Bar Mitzvah training program by allowing exceptional students to
read Torah portions at their Bar Mitzvah celebrations. This has now become
the standard for the best trained of our Bar Mitzvah candidates. It is inter¬
esting to note that children have taken this challenge to read more than
expected.
Another area of turning a lack into an advantage is the preparation
of local materials. Textbooks for Jewish education often reflect too many
diverse viewpoints. In order to adapt them to our school, our staff was chal¬
lenged to write workbooks which would meet our local needs. This will
create a situation of greater living experiences. Because of this approach,
new courses were added to our curriculum such as Philsophy to the Sunday
High School Department and Classical Literature to the Hebrew High
School Department.
(Continued on Page 77)
DR. NATHAN
SCHNEIDER,
Co-Chairman

uJudaism — ‘The religious doctrines and rite of the Jews’ as Webster defines it —
and we, its adherents, have survived principally because of every generation there was
a shearit yisroel, a remnant within Israel, which had the conviction, the love
and fortitude, to cling and cleave to Torah and tradition, loyally and with determin¬
ation. Even ‘unto death’ this saving remnant held true to the Torah and walked stead¬
fastly in its ways. Therefore I submit, because of those who were the martyrs, as well
as those who survived, we live. We live because they taught us that there is purpose
to life.”
Charles Rosengarten

The Daily Kindergarten and Nursery conducts sessions throughout the school year composed of the following
staff: Mrs. M. J. Reiser, Mrs. Carl Hackel, Mrs. Harry Jaffe and Mrs. Edward Sussman. Each of the above
teachers have been with our school system for many years and are well qualified for their respective classes.
The Kindergarten and Nursery meets daily Monday through Friday from 9 a.m. to 12 noon with transporta¬
tion provided. A mid-morning snack is served to each child and a playground program is also included each day.
Several plays are presented throughout the year depicting the various Jewish Holidays in conjunction with
Open House for parents and visitation of the classrooms.
The two oldest classes of Kindegarten pictured with veteran teachers Mrs. Carl Hackel and Mrs. M. J. Reiser.
The majority of these two groups comprise the Graduating Class of 1965. These youngsters receive instructions
in Jewish festivals and customs, with a Kiddush planned on Fridays to usher in the Sabbath.

1964-65 Teaching Staff: Seated: Mrs. Leo Mos¬
kovitz, Director, Mr. and Mrs. Leon Zucker. Stand¬
ing: Mrs. Nathan Sandler, Mrs. Herbert Plotkin,
Mrs. Perry Mibab.
Now in its fourth year of operation, the Center
Foundation School was recently recognized by
the United Synagogue as an approved Solomon
Schechter Day School. With this high recognition
the Day School will assume the new name com¬
mencing with the 1965-66 term, which will also
initiate the fourth and fifth grades.
“In 1956 two Solomon Schechter Day Schools were opened,
one under the auspices of the Philadelphia branches of the
United Synagogue and the Rabbinical Assembly, and another
under the joint auspices of nine Conservative congregations
in Central Queens, New York City. A third day school was
organized that year by the East Midwood Jewish Center.
“Other day schools had been organized in previous years by
Temple Beth El of Rockaway Park, New York; Anshe Emet
of Chicago; Shaare Zion of Montreal; and Adath Israel of
Montreal. In addition, foundation schools were being con¬
ducted by congregations in Jacksonville, Florida; Houston,
Texas; Staten Island, New York; and Toronto, Canada
from: A History of the United Synagogue
of America

Youth Activities
At the Center a great deal of emphasis is placed on the proper training
and direction of the current youth in the congregation today, and for that
reason there are Center youth clubs and organizations for children of every
age level.
Always striving to improve our youth program, the Youth Committee
this past year added to its staff the part time services of George Glossman
to assist with the over-all youth program at our Synagogue. Under the super¬
vision of Robert Fischer and the assistance of George Glossman, our youth
program is destined to even greater heights during the coming year. The
following lay people are serving in various posts to plan for the youth of our
congregation:
SSSSBSK sIÍTOMWWWBmBWBI
ROBERT FISCHER
DR. HARRY I. YERGIN
Dr. Harry Yergin, a past Cubmaster and
Scoutmaster and father of very active USY
members, serves as the Chairman of the Cen¬
ter Youth Commission and in that capacity
coordinates all youth activities and sets
standards for future goals. Representing the
Sisterhood on the Youth Committee is Mrs.
Leon Schinasi, a former Girl Scout Chair¬
man. Her organization helps to sponsor and
finance part of our youth program.
With the cooperation of such devoted vol¬
unteer advisors as S. Robert Shorstein, Rita
Weinstein, Rene Slott and Evelyn Brauner,
the USY program continues to show improve¬
ment in every endeavor.
GEORGE GLOSSMAN
C3e*iier
o
raanizations
CENTER YOUTH LEAGUE (CYL) — A co-ed USY Chap-
ter for 11th and 12th graders, meeting every 2nd and 4th
Sunday with varied programs from September to May.
CHARLES PALOT CLUB (CPC) — USY Chapter for 9th
and 10th grade boys and girls, meeting throughout the
school year on the 2nd and 4th Sunday at the Center.
MAJOR’S PRE-TEENS — A co-ed youth club for 7th and
8th graders with its programs geared to this pre-teen
level with varied social, religious and athletic functions
held monthly.
TWEEN-AGERS — Boys and girls of the 5th and 6th grades
participate monthly in this, our youngest club.
To this list is also added our sponsorship of BOY SCOUTS
(Troop 14), CUB SCOUTS (Pack 14), GIRL SCOUTS
(Troops 6 & 193) and BROWNIE TROOPS (219 & 269).

1965-66 CYL OFFICERS
Standing, Jack Mizrahi, 1st Vice-President; Leonard Selber, President; Miss Rita
Weinstein, Advisor; Donald Safer, Treasurer. Seated: Beverly Hart, 3rd Vice-Presi¬
dent; Mendy Marks, 2nd Vice-President; Dolly Fleet, Corresponding Secretary and
Peggy Cohen, Recording Secretary.
C. Y. L. (Center Youth League) is an organization affil¬
iated with United Synagogue Youth, whose main interests
are closely associated with the Synagogue plus a varied
program of cultural, social and athletic activities.
The total aims of the USY program itself may be sum¬
med up in the words “Bring the Youth Back to the Syna¬
gogue.”
This past year was a busy one for our USY’ers with the
upcoming year even more active. Opening the season the
returning LTI’ers presented a skit learned at the ten day
encampment. From there our young people went on a
round of meetings, social activities, a car wash and a suc¬
cessful macaroon sale for Passover.
The second Annual National Send-Off Convention in
December showed our USY’ers at their best. The Conven-
Richard Browdy, last year’s recipient of the Best USY’er
Award, presenting the same award to Leonard Selber and
their Advisor Rita Weinstein presenting the Best Girl
USY’er Award to Judy Horovitz.
A scene from the play, “Saturday at the Cohens.” Left
to right, Judy Horovitz, Richard Browdy, Phyllis Glossman,
Jack Mizrahi, Sheila Kramer, Charles Slott, Anita Mesnick,
and Cheryl Sussman.
tion featured ice-skating at the Coliseum, several interesting
workshops and an informative talk by Philip Selber.
In March a delegation journeyed to Miami by chartered
bus to attend the Regional Convention. This was climaxed
by one of our local USY’ers, Jack Mizrahi, being awarded
an Israeli Pilgrimage.
The high point of the year was the annual USY Banquet in
May. The USY Thespians presented “Saturday at the
Cohens” to a large and receptive audience under the direc¬
tion of Mrs. M. J. Reiser. The J-Notes provided the music
for dancing.
Judy Horovitz and Leonard Selber received awards as the
outstanding girl and boy USY’er for 1964-65. Miss Rita
Weinstein will continue to serve as Advisor for 1965-66.

1965-66 OFFICERS OF C. P. C.
Standing: Jack Bettman, 3rd Vice-President; Eliot Safer, President; Morry Edwards,

Anotuer banner year in the annals of the Charles^!
Palot Club (CPC) group has been successfully re¬
corded.
To supplement the activities listed by CYL, CPC
played a great part in promoting the Foundation
School Hootenanny in October, which sponsored the
well-known folk singer Abraham Grobard. Over 75
USY’ers came in from Daytona Beach and Orlando
to attend the Hootenanny and a Swim Party after¬
ward at the Campsite.
At the second Annual Send-Off, CPC joined CYL
in raising over $300 for the National Building Spiritual
Bridges project.
At the Regional Convention held in Miami Beach,
CPC was one of the few chapters to receive a Re¬
gional Chapter Award for outstanding achievement.
The following represented CPC at the 1965 Leader¬
ship Training Institute, August 18-26 at Camp Blue
Star in Hendersonville, N. C.; Jack Bettman, Jeff
Goldstein, Janice Hoffenberg, Debbie Morris, Lynelle
Resnick, Eliot Safer, Donna Savage, Randy Silverberg,
Sherry Silverman and Walter Slaff.

Boy Scout Troop Pictured with Dr. Harry
With a change of Scoutmasters, the Center Boy
Scout Troop continued its varied and full program.
Henry Lasris became the new Scoutmaster, with Dr.
Harry Yergin stepping up to Youth Committee Chair¬
man.
The Troop continues to be among the top outfits
in the Mohawk District with the following accom¬
plishments during 1964-65:
Several overnight hikes during the year included
trips to Ocala for the annual Camporee, a trip to
Camp Echokotee, a short-lived Camporee at Lake
Hampton and a weekend at the Center Campsite.
Eight boys received the Ner Tamid award in special
ceremonies during Scout Sabbath this past spring:
Earl Berman, Charles Trager, Ricky Goldstein, Lee
Lasris, Ira Price, Marc Gradman, Jeff Goldstein,
Randy Silverberg. Dr. Yergin again deserves many
thanks for working with this class.
Martin Berman and Gary Wolfson became Eagle
Scouts in May 1962.
1965 Ner Tamid Award Recipients with Rabbi Turetsky,
Dr. Yergin and Henry Lasris
Yergin and Henry Lasris, Scoutmaster
Ted Freedman, Institutional Representative
and Dr. Harry Yergin shown at the
Annual Scout Banquet.
Troop 14 sent 18 boys to a week at Camp Echokotee
where they studied scouting requirements both for
advancement and merit badges. While at Camp, Jeff
Goldstein and Scoutmaster Henry Lasris were tapped
out for the Order of the Arrow. This is an honor
group which a boy may join only if elected by mem¬
bers of his troop. The troop made arrangements for
kosher food this year, courtesy of the Center and the
delicious cooking of Annie Mae. Needless to say we
were the envy of the camp which found the counselors
competing for the privilege of being our guest at each
meal.
Three weeks later, Gary Wolfson, Martin Berman,
Jeffrey Goldstein, Charles Trager, Ira Price, and
Marc Gradman joined a group of Scouts for a trip to
the much heralded Camp Philmont in Arizona. This is
a rugged two week session of hiking in the mountains
where they carry everything they need on their backs.
Troop 14 is looking forward to another active year.
Any boy of age 11 or more is invited to join

couts
Cub Pack 14 with Scoutmaster, Sidney Engelhardt
The enthusiasm and devotion shown by our Den Mothers and Cub
Scouts has lifted our Pack among the leaders in the city. The 41 Cubs
between the ages of 8 through 10 participate in weekly Den meetings at
the respective Den Mothers’ homes with monthly Pack Meetings held on
the last Thursday of the month at the Center.
Special thanks are due to the devotion and dedication of Cubmaster
Sidney Engelhardt, Assistant Cubmaster Harry Kowitz and Mrs. Jerome
Fink, Chief Den Mother. These leaders, along with the support and strength
of the Den Mothers and Pack Committee, have paved the way of Pack 14
as the number one Pack in the North Florida Council.

MRS. JACK PRICE
Girl Scout Chairman for 1965-66
Brownie Troop No. 210 with leaders,
Mrs. Albert Datz and Mrs. Sheldon Gendzier
Our Center’s Girl Scout and Brownie Troops under the
Chairmanship of Mrs. Howard Hecht, consisting of 85 girls,
had an extremely active year. Special thanks are due to the
Troop Leaders and Co-Leaders shown with their Troops below.
The Brownies were busy making tray favors for Hope
Haven Hospital, and working in a Booth for the Sisterhood’s
Hanukah Fair. The Jr. Girl Scouts collected canned goods and
foods for needy Jewish families. For World Thinking Day our
scouts presented “Dolls for Democracy”, a program designed
to create interest in Girl Scouts throughout the world. The
Cadette troop was busy with community projects such as
volunteer work for the Nalpo Children’s Home, stuffing
envelopes for the T. B. program, visiting Tallahassee while
working on their government badge, donating tray favors for
the Duval Medical Center, and sewing a patch quilt for pre-
centation at the Civic Auditorium Home Making Fair.
Our Girl Scout Banquet this past year was enthusiasti¬
cally received and brought to light the many accomplishments
of our Scouts.
Not Pictured is Brownie
Troop No. 269. Leaders
are Mrs. Abe Fraden and
Mrs. Hilbert Margol.
Jr. Girl Scouts, Troop No. 193, shown with leaders,
Mrs. Jack Price, Mrs. Dave Silver and Mrs. Sam Blumberg.

1965 Chenier
Byron Morris, Co-Chairman
Robert Lasris, Co-Chairman
Completing the 20th season of the Center Day
Camp this past August 13 were over 200 youngsters
and a staff of 64. With the conclusion of the nine-week
camping session, the highlight was the annual Award
Night with the following campers receiving the best
camper awards in their respective groups: Tadpoles
Boys, Bradley Hacker; Girls, Leslie Esman; Midget
Boys, Richie Maslow; Girls, Phyllis Silver; Junior
Boys, Joel Morris; Girls, Jeri Jarchower; Senior Boys,
Scott Baker; Girls, Pam Fayne.
Instituted this past year were athletic patches
which campers received for mastering various sports,
in addition to the best camper trophies listed above.
Judo and Karatee were added to our diversified
program and much enthusiasm was shown by the older
boys and the junior girls for this defensive art. The
closing weeks included Color War and outings to
places of interest. The staff and the Day Camp Com¬
mittee look forward to their third year at the new
36 acre Campsite with many added improvements and
the constant addition of new and varied activities to
Our Counselor-in-training Class with their Instructor, Mrs.
Linda Fischer. The Best C.I.T. awards for the boys went to
Beanie Caslar, Mark Rosner and Mark Becker. The girls awards
were copped by Janice Hoffenberg, Sheila Mesnick and Beth
Lesnik.
our schedule. The four campers swimming three miles
during the summer were Doug Engel, Diane Dreher,
Marc Grauer, and Scott Baker.

MR. BEN CASLAR, President
With its dual goal of spreading friendship and
enabling married couples to share good times, the
Mr. and Mrs. Club is now in its 12th successful year.
Each month, exciting and novel affairs are held and
guided through the leadership of committees formed
to arrange each gala event.
The themes vary from month to month, but tradi¬
tional events include an extravagant New Year's Eve
party, a mid-year Installation Banquet, and a family
picnic and fun day during the summer months.
As in all other social clubs, there is a central
core of officers and board members, and the organiza¬
tion is governed by husbands and wives serving and
working together. Through each member's sharing
the responsibility of serving on a monthly committee,
an atmosphere of warm congeniality always prevails,
and the promotion of good fellowship is the end result.
All couples who are interested in joining and
sharing all the wonderful things offered by the Mr.
and Mrs. Club are urged to contact any of the club's
officers or board members.
mi mi ■■■■■ini inn iiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiiii
Mrs. Marsha Berdit, Mrs. Molly Tierney, Mrs. Evelyn Talisman
The following are the officers for the 1965-66
season: Mollie S. Tierney, President; Frank W. Ber¬
man, Vice-President; Marsha E. Berdit, Treasurer and
Evelyn Talisman, Secretary.
l ^Jantes
Knights & Dames was organized in 1964 for the
purpose of furnishing a social outlet for single Jewish
adults. Socials are held every six to eight weeks. Invi¬
tations are sent out to single Jewish adults and adult
groups in neighboring cities of Florida and Georgia.
Anyone who is interested in joining this group
or who knows of anyone who might be interested in
this organization is invited to contact any of the
officers or members in order that their names may
be added to the mailing list.
Highlight of the past season was a weekend party
at the Thunderbird Motel. The attendance included
single Jewish adults from as far north as Atlanta, Ga.
and as far south as Miami, Fla. Reciprocal invitations
have been extended from the Savannah and Atlanta
single adult groups.

The Amity Club enjoyed a very successful season
last year with various monthly affairs at the Center
climaxed with the June Banquet. The members en¬
joyed outstanding activities outside the Center such
as the Ice Show at the Coliseum, Jai Alai at Daytona
Beach, and New Years Eve at the Mayflower Hotel.
The Amity Club is a social club for married
couples of the Jewish faith and we welcome inquiries.
If interested, please call one of the above officers or
board members.

For the 5th year, the 32 Club serves as a social
pinnacle for the young Jewish couples of Jacksonville.
One member of the couple must be under 32 years of
age to join the club but once you are a member there
is no age limit. We try to vary our monthly affairs to
include all types of desirable entertainment. Each
Board Member is Chairman of one affair and the type
of affair is limited only to his imagination and the
budget allotted to his committee per year.
Any interested couples should contact Barry Zisser,
membership chairman, or any officer or board member.

"I regard the centrality of the Synagogue In Jewish
life not so much In organizational terms as in the implication
that the Synagogue is the basic and primary unit of Jewish
organization. This means that every Jew regardless of his
other associations and activities should associate himself
with a Synagogue in his community. More important still,
the spirit of the Synagogue and the influence of its teach¬
ings should be incorporated into and be an active influence
in the I ives of the men and women, lay and professional, who
guide the destinies of these other Jewish communal organ¬
izations and furnish the Jewish communal leadership."
MAXWELL ABBELL

Serving the entire Jewish community of Jackson¬
ville, the Jewish Community Council is the central
Welfare Fund and Community Relations body of the
Jewish community. An association of individuals,
synagogues, organizations and social service agencies,
the program encompasses key activities which give
unity, cohesion and coordination to local Jewish com¬
munal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Cam¬
paign provides support to significant local, national
and overseas organizations.The National United Jew¬
ish Appeal has expressed American Jewry's respon¬
sibility for the welfare and rehabilitation needs of
Jewish communities the world over for over 20 years.
River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged and the
Jewish Welfare Society are the local beneficaries.
The Council publishes a monthly newspaper known
as the Commentator. It is the official organ of the
Council and its member organizations.
The Council conducts a leisure time activities
program which includes Youth Council programs such
as college preparatory workshops, a youth news page
in the Commentator, and a youth division in the an¬
nual Jewish Welfare Fund Campaign. The Council
also sponsors a community summer Day Camp for
children between the ages of 4 and 15.

Honorary President Arthur J. Gutman
Aging is fast becoming everyone’s business as the miracles
of modern medicine extend the life span beyond the biblical
three score and ten. The startling facts are that there are now
over 10,000 individuals in the United States whose verified
age is a hundred plus.
The implications and impact on family and community
Samuel J. Thier, President
responsibility become very significant since most people reach¬
ing the 8th decade of their lives begin to need help in their
daily patterns of living as well as more medical care. When
these needs become more than the family, or the community,
can provide River Garden stands ready to serve with its highly
specialized professional program.
Programming at the Home means more than the medical¬
nursing services of a hospital. It incorporates a broad gamut
of activities to enrich the lives of the residents to give them
that intangible motivation that life can still have meaning
despite the handicaps and debility of illnesses. Even mental
and emotional changes which occur so frequently in the aged
are accepted as a process of decline which can well be man¬
aged at River Garden. The success of this phase of the pro¬
gram can well be measured by the fact that very few aged
patients of Jewish faith are in the Florida State Hospitals.
Providing a satisfactory way of life for the aging in a
society which is youth oriented is indeed a challenge. The
problems are deeply embedded in the whole fabric of the
American culture. In its limited scope of caring for those who
cannot care for themselves, River Garden has evolved a philo¬
sophy of care which embodies the basic values of the Torah
and the highly developed professionalism of today.

¿dCadies CTCelrew ¿Sheltering ¿Slid ¿Society
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Vice-President Mrs. I. P. Edwards
Vice-President Mrs. Myron Rosenberg
Vice-President Mrs. J. Paul Witten
Recording Secretary Mrs. Larry Richter

Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Lazarus

Treasurer (Projects) Mrs. Louis Witten
Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society is entering
its 42nd year of giving “Service with a Heart". Our
revered founders’ dream of establishing a resident
home for the aged of our people became a realty in
1946, and since that time our role has been primarily
that of an auxiliary to River Garden Home for the
Aged.
We provide the extra budgetary needs as fur¬
nishing, redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen and
informary equipment and hobby shop materials. Funds
are raised through our Tombola, Donor and social
printing projects, as well as dues and life member¬
ships. Linens at the Home are replenished through
our annual Linen Shower affair.
In the field of service, Ladies Aid sponsors ac¬
tivity and volunteer programs. Residents participate
in projects such as ceramic and art classes, hobby
shop, resident’s magazine, and the newly formed
Spanish class.
Personal service to the residents is rendered by
our Grey Ladies and our Gardenettes, a teen-age
corps of volunteers who serve on week-ends and school
Officers of Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid
vacations. Our members also provide door-to-door
transportation for non-residents to attend the weekly
Golden Sunshine meetings held at River Garden and
continue to lend our support and guidance to the
group. On special holidays our women provide enter¬
tainment and refreshments for the residents.
We pledge our continued devotion and service to
River Garden and its residents in the hope that we
can help bring some measure of comfort and mean¬
ing to their lives.

Mr. Lawrence Rackow,

Founded in 1917, the Jewish Family and Children’s Serv¬
ices has been assisting its clients solve family and personal
problems for the past 48 years. Last year some 300 different
families or individuals were served in one or more programs
offered hy the agency. The placement of five infants for adop¬
tion in 1964 set an all time high in this area for the agency
and spurred the search for prospective adoption applicants.
We also look for and were successful in our search for short¬
term foster homes, “big brothers” and “big sisters”. The largest
portion of our “caseload”, some 40%, came from the oldest
age groups.
Although the problems dealt with do not neatly fit
themselves into categories and often come in bunches with
ramifications in many directions, the agency’s work might be
better understood by a listing of the services offered.
1. Personal Counseling
2. Adoption Program
3. Foster Home Program
4. Assistance to the Aged
5. Employment and
Vocational Services
6. New American
Resettlement Program
7. Short Term Financial
Assistance
8. Transient Program
9. Prison Program
10.Day Camp and College
Scholarship Program
The understanding and cooperation of the Jewish com¬
munity is vital to insure the best services. The response in
terms of donations of time, clothing, and money continues to
be excellent. We need more volunteers for our Friendly Visit¬
ing Committee and quicker alerts about employment oppor-
tuities. Remember, too, that your whole-hearted support of the
LTnited Community Services and the Jewish Welfare Fund
drives make this work possible.

Ex officio I. M. Lieberman
The essential function of the Zionist Organization
of America is to keep alive in this country an interest
in the State of Israel. This interest is many-sided, as
the efforts of Zionism always were. The political ob¬
jective was the creation of a Jewish State. To this end
it was necessary to revive the Hebrew language, ac¬
quire land, train colonists for settlement and raise
funds to finance the project. All this the Zionist
movement did.
Myer Cohen, President
Today, the Z.O.A. continues to foster support for
the State of Israel. Moreover, the Z.O.A. seeks to
strengthen the bonds between American Jewry and
the Jewish State on the basis of the cultural tradi¬
tion that is our common heritage.
Religious differencs present no obstacle as Zionism
encompasses all shades of religious belief in every
global area.
The Z.O.A. invites all Jewish men to join its ranks.
^Ueteran

Senior Hadassah
President Mrs. Melvin Barnert
Vice-President (Fund Raising) Mrs. Maurice Goldstein
Vice-President (Program) Mrs. David Goodman
Vice-President (Membership) Mrs. Bernard Richter
Vice-President (Education) Mrs. David Lewis
Treasurer Mrs. Jack Shorstein
Recording Secretary Mrs. M. D. Evans
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Stephen Puldy
Financial Secretary Mrs. Mac Presser
Hadassah was founded in 1912 by Henrietta Szold, and
from a mere handful of dedicated American women, has grown
to a membership of over 318,000. The local chapter was formed
in 1925.
Hadassah projects are actively functioning on two far-
flung horizons. On the American scene, an educational pro¬
gram, a youth activities program, the Young Judeans and the
Junior Hadassah, Hadassah co-sponsors with Z.O.A. Camp
Judaea in Hendersonville, North Carolina. In Israel, Hadassah
encompasses Hadassah Medical Organization, Medical Cen¬
ter, Youth Aliyah, Vocational education and the Jewish Na¬
tional Fund. By helping Israel to help itself, so that it can
take its place with dignity among the freedom-loving nations
of the world, Hadassah reaffirms the Jewish faith in the
heritage of freedom.
Two groups comprise the Jacksonville Chapter: The
Senior Hadassah and the Hannah Senesch Evening group.
111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111 ii 111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111111
OFFICERS OF SENIOR HADASSAH
Almost 750 women boast membership in the local chapter,
and annually seek to raise quotas totaling nearly $17,500 in
order to meet commitments in Israel, as well as in America.
Senior Hadassah meets every Tuesday of each month
from September through May. This coming year’s calendar
offers a variety of activities with a well-rounded educational
and social program to interest every member.

as well as non-working women for whom evening meetings

Successful fund raising efforts for Hadassah’s vast medical
and humanitarian activities in many parts of the world,
coupled with congeniality at meetings, makes this an ideal
group to join.
Meetings in the Jacksonville Jewish Center are held at
8 p.m. on the second Monday of each month.

Young Judaea is a movement dedicated to developing generations of American
Jewish youth rooted by their heritage and devoted to attaining self fulfillment as

others went to Camp Judaea in Hendersonville, N. C.

The members of the Jacksonville Section of the
National Council of Jewish Women give to the com¬
munity their time and whole-hearted efforts in a
variety of needed services. We ask the community
to help us with the means to carry these projects for¬
ward:
OFFICERS OF N. C. J. W.
1. Juvenile Shelter — We are the only civic group
offering planned, year-round programs and activi¬
ties for children who desperately need help.
2. Mental Health Fair — Our annual program and
seminar, jointly sponsored with the Mental Health
Association of Duval County, is designed to in¬
crease public understanding in this field.
3. Teachers’ Aid — In cooperation with the excep¬
tional children’s department of the Duval County
Board of Public Instruction, our trained members
aid classroom teachers with academic instruction
of children with speech and hearing handicaps. In
addition, our Tribute Fund donated four hearing
aids for indigent children last year.
4. Women in Community Services (WICS) — We
screen underprivileged girls ages 16 thru 21 for
the Job Corps Training Centers.
5. Sewing Committee — We make layettes for dis¬
tribution to the needy.
6. Medical Loan Shelf — Through the Visiting Nurse
Association, we supply hospital and sick room
equipment to the indigent in our community.
7. Senior Citizens Program — We sponsor “Education
Day” for Senior Citizens. Leading doctors and
social workers conduct this free program for all
Senior Citizens groups.
Our members are most willing to continue these
services to the community. We feel that the commun¬
ity is just as willing to support us in continuing and
expanding our services. Therefore, during our annual
fund raising drive, we appeal to you for a contribu¬
tion.

B’nai B’rith today is a world-wide organization of many facets, re¬
spected and revered by all, and joined with dignity by Jews everywhere.
JVo. 800)
EDWARD MASS, President
The following are some of the many and varied
facets of B’nai B’rith:
1. Contribution to citizenship by training youth, coopera¬
tion with other groups.
2. Hillel Foundation — the means of moving the young
people on campus back to Jewish life.
3. Vocational service — assisting Jews toward economic
development.
4. B’nai B’rith Youth Organization — Founded in 1924
because of a concern over the vast number of young
Jews who were drifting away from Judaism. The leaders
of tomorrow are trained in this organization.
4. Patriotism — B’nai B’rith went into action from the
Civil War through each of the conflicts. Volunteer work¬
ers served in bringing hope and rehabilitation to the
armed forces and veterans.
6. The Anti-Defamation League, Defender of Rights, was
founded in 1913 by a brilliant lawyer. His theory of
understanding through enlightenment is the foundation
for the League’s approach to current problems. Must
men hate?
“The cycle of Brotherly Love, Benevolence and Harmony
will be never ending.”
The membership of the local A. B. Weil Lodge increased
leaps and bounds last year under the able chairmanship of
Hyman Selber with over 200 members on our rolls.
Leonard Barnett, A. D. L. Chairman, and his committee
worked to such a degree that our lodge has been recognized
by the entire district.
Victor Hubsch, the loyal and tireless financial secretary
made possible much of the success this year.
Myron Blattner, V. P. in charge of A. Z. A., counselled his
group of young men very successfully. Lionel Gaines, Emanuel
Ehrlich, Bud Goldberg, Arthur Hurwitz, Alfred Rosenzweig
and the rest of the Board all helped to make this an out¬
standing administration.
No greater reward can be received than the joy and satis¬
faction resulting from service to our brothers and sisters. By
such efforts our own B’nai B’rith and the entire community
will benefit.
ED MASS, President

This past year, under the able leadership of Aleph Godol’s Mark Hipler and
Howard Rosenblatt, Gators A.Z.A. reached new heights. Our chapter reached highest
limits and sent delegates to four conventions. Succeeding Jeff Bootzin on the North
Florida Board was the new Council Pledgemaster Elliot Borkson, newly elected Gator
Aleph Godol.
Jeff Bootzin received AZA’s highest award, Silver Shield of David. Howard Rosen¬
blatt again won the regional oratory contest and teamed with Jeff Bootzin won the
regional debate. This is the third year in a row that a Gator AZA has won the oratory
contest and have never been defeated in debate.
The chapter activities were varied and lived up to the five-fold program. Under
the religious fold, we hosted three Sabbaths, one at the Temple and two at the Jewish
Center; under cultural, we showed films of various health organizations; under com¬
munity service, we collected contributions for Muscular Dystrophy and aided in the
ALSÁC drive; in athletics, we participated in the Jewish Community Council basket¬
ball league and played Esquire Club in football and last but not least the social end,
we held at least one social a month with the girls in Gator’s BBG, the sister organi¬
zation, and conducted our first annual Stag Party May 1st at the Jewish Center
Campsite.

OFFICERS OF B’NAI B’RITH WOMEN
B’nai B’rith Women is synonomous with its motto '‘Service
Fidelity and DevotionThere are many philanthropies which
rely on B’nai B’rith Women for their support.
In America, they are National Jewish Hospital in Denver,
Colo., Bellefaire Children’s Home in Cleveland, Ohio, Lee N.
Levi Memorial Hospital in Hot Springs, Ark., B’nai B’rith
Family Center at Mayo Clinic, and the Salk Foundation.
In Israel, B’nai B’rith sponsors two Kibbutzim, Children’s
Home, Martyr’s Forest and Hillel Foundations.
In Duval County, we have several projects at River Garden
Hebrew Home for the Aged, Duval Medical Center, Sunny
Acres Park, United Jewish Appeal and Israel Bond Drive;
and at Lake City, philanthropy at the Veterans Administration
Hospital, as well as assorted drives under Citizenship, Vet¬
erans and Community Affairs.
Every year we distribute 1500 Calendar Memo Date Books
to the public schools through the Anti-Defamation League in
conjunction with the Jewish Community Council. Another
successful program has been “The Dolls for Democracy” pro¬
gram.

BARBARA FRANZBLAU
President
We had a very active year in Gators B.B.G., beginning with
the MIT (Members in Training) Tea. B.B.G. engaged in many
money-making projects, brownie sales, fashion show and
fashions by Rosenblum’s, a hootenanny and a raffle. We also
had many social events, including bowling, two surprise break¬
fasts, a May Day and a slumber party.
Gators also donated their time for many worthy causes in¬
cluding Leukemia Drive, Muscular Dystrophy Drive and on
Halloween night collected candy for children in hospitals.
On Nov. 27-29, 1964 we had a Social Weekend in Orlando,
Dec. 27-31 the Regional Convention was held in Miami. Gators
was proud to receive first place in the oratory contest, second
place in the newspaper and several prizes in the song con¬
tests. Susan Hilsenrad, the Chapter President, was elected
Regional President, a great honor for the local chapter. The
next Convention took place in St. Petersburg.
Religion played a big part in the activities with two B.B.Y.O.
Sabbaths in which the girls participated. For the first time
we took part in the Safety Program. June 13, 1965 was the
annual Mother-Daughter Installation Dinner. We ended a
tremendous year with a Bonfire at which time life member¬
ships were presented.

The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the
Jewish Progressive Society is organized to encourage
thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium where¬
by members can invest and accumulate savings. The
Credit Union carries life savings insurance of $2,000
and loan protection insurance without any direct cost
to the members. Last year the Board declared a 3%
dividend on savings and 10% rebate of interest to
borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The
Member is the most important person in our Credit
Union. The Member is not an outsider, he is an owner
of our Credit Union. The Member is not an interrup¬
tion of our work, he is the purpose of it. The Member
does us a favor when he calls — We are not doing
Officers of Progressive Credit Union
him a favor when we serve him. The Member is not
a cold statistic — He is a human being with feelings
and emotions like our own. The Member is not some¬
one to argue or match wits with. The Member is a
person who brings us his wants — it is our job to
fill these wants. The Member is deserving of the most
courteous and attentive treatment we can give him.
The Member is not dependent on us, we are dependent
on him. The Member is the lifeblood of this Credit
Union.
The year’s activities climax with the annual dinner
meeting at the Center. Anyone who wishes to join
is extended a cordial invitation.

YOM KIPPUR (Day of Atonement) is a day of fasting and prayer when we strive to reawaken the latent spiritual
power in our souls so that it may become an effective and directing force in our daily conduct.
SUKKOT (Feast of Thanksgiving) is the Jewish festival of thanksgiving on the occasion of the final ingathering of the
harvest. The Sukkah symbolizes the wanderings of our ancestors in the wilderness of Sinai.

8 TEVET
HANUKAH (Dedication) — Anniversary of the day when the
Jews, led by Judah Maccabee and his brothers, freed the Temple
from Syrian invaders. At the Temple's rededication, only enough
undefiled oil was found to light the Holy Lamp for one day. Mira¬
culously, it burned eight days. Thus, on the first day of Hanukah,
Jews light one candle of the Menorah, adding another flame each
day until all eight are lit.

HAMISHA ASAR B'SHEVAT (Fifteenth Day of Shevat), the New Year of the Trees. Often referred to
as the Jewish Arbor Day, this holiday commemorates the ancient need for trees in the Holy Land. It
is clebrated everywhere with appropriate songs and games. Fruits long associates with the trees that
once grew in the Holy Land (and have begun to grow there again) are eaten on this day.

Ahare-Kedoshim
10 IYAR
PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread — an eight-day
celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from Egyptian slavery.
During the traditional Seder meal, on the first two nights of Pass-
over, Jewish families read the Haggadah (a ritual recitation of the
story of the Exodus. Throughout the Passover periods, foods are
selected according to special dietary laws. Intead of bread, Jews
eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from
Ecfypt, when there was no time to allow the bread to rise.

SHAVUOT — On this day, the Ten Commandments are read in the synagogue to
commemorate the giving of the Law (Torah) to Moses on Mount Sinai. Shavuot
also marks the early wheat harvest in the Holy Land. On this holiday Jews decorate
their homes and synagogues with greens and flowers.
